    Discovery of Novel Plasmodium falciparum HDAC1 Inhibitors with Dual-Stage Antimalarial Potency and Improved Safety Based on the Clinical Anticancer Drug Candidate Quisinostat

    Abstract

    Previously, we identified the clinical anticancer drug candidate quisinostat as a novel and potent antimalarial lead compound. To further enhance the antimalarial effect and improve safety, 31 novel spirocyclic hydroxamic acid derivatives were synthesized based on the structure of quisinostat, and their antimalarial activities and cytotoxicity were evaluated. Among them, compound 11 displayed broad potency in vitro against several multiresistant malarial parasites, especially two artemisinin-resistant clinical isolates. Moreover, 11 could eliminate both liver and erythrocytic parasites in vivo, kill all morphological erythrocytic parasites with specific potency against schizonts, and show acceptable metabolic stability and pharmacokinetic properties. Western blot analysis, PfHDAC gene knockdown, and enzymatic inhibition experiments collectively confirmed that PfHDAC1 was the target of 11. In summary, 11 is a structurally novel PfHDAC1 inhibitor with the potential to prevent and cure malaria, overcome multidrug resistance, and provide a prospective prototype for antimalarial drug research.

    Â Note

    Originally published ASAP on February 4, 2021; Figure 3 revised February 9, 2021.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Malaria is a mosquito-transmitted severe infectious disease caused by Plasmodium parasites. The World Health Organization (WHO) estimated that in 2018, malaria caused 228 million cases of disease and 405,000 deaths globally, most of which were induced by Plasmodium falciparum (P. falciparum).(1) Since no vaccine is currently available, chemical therapies are still the primary methods for treating and preventing malaria. After the pioneering discovery of quinine, several antimalarial drugs have been developed and launched into the campaign against malaria worldwide.(2) Currently, five artemisinin-based combination therapies (ACTs) are the only WHO-recommended first-line drug therapies for malaria.(3) However, malaria parasites have developed resistance against nearly all drugs.(4) ACT-resistant malaria parasites derived from Cambodia have recently compromised the efficacy of malaria treatment in the Greater Mekong subregion.(5) Although remedial measures can alleviate the resistance dilemma,(6) the prospects for malaria treatment remain worrisome. Therefore, the development of novel antimalarial drugs is an urgent requirement for solving resistance problems and controlling malaria worldwide in the future.
    As a critical epigenetic modulator of gene expression, histone deacetylase (HDAC) has long been involved in the pathogenesis and therapeutic investigation of many other human diseases, and four anticancer HDAC inhibitors have been approved by the U.S. FDA thus far (Figure 1).(7) Currently, five P. falciparum HDACs (PfHDACs) have been identified in parasites, including class I-type pfHDAC1 and class II-type PfHDAC2/3, which contain Zn2+ cations as a cofactor, and class III-type PfSir2A/2B, which contain NAD+ cations as a cofactor.(8) They can regulate the acetylation level of both malarial histone and nonhistone proteins and play critical roles in the survival and reproduction of parasites.(8â16) Therefore, the inhibition of PfHDAC may provide a potential approach for malarial treatment.(8) Since the pioneering discovery of Apicidin in 1996,(17) several groups have reported the multistage therapeutic potency of PfHDAC inhibitors.(8) However, no PfHDAC inhibitors have successfully reached clinical treatment or study despite more than two decades of research. Although several PfHDAC inhibitors displayed antimalarial potency in vitro, only a few compounds have been tested for erythrocytic therapeutic effects in vivo,(17â22) and currently, no PfHDAC inhibitor has been investigated for liver-stage therapeutic effects in vivo. Since the inhibition of PfSir2A/2B resulted only in weak antimalarial activity, most researchers have focused on inhibition studies on Zn2+-containing PfHDACs.(8) However, because of the difficulties of obtaining recombinant PfHDAC protein samples, in previous reports, most compounds have been tested only for pan-PfHDAC inhibitory effects, and only a few compounds have been tested for inhibition against PfHDAC1(23â26) since this was the only available isolated PfHDAC protein. Overall, detailed exploration of therapeutic effects in vivo, target elucidation and the inhibition of different PfHDAC subtypes are still unsolved problems in antimalarial HDAC inhibitor research.
    In previous work, we identified the class I and II-type human HDAC inhibitor quisinostat (1, Scheme 1), an anticancer drug candidate entering phase II clinical trials, as a novel antimalarial compound with prominent in vitro activity identified by screening an epigenetic inhibitor library.(27)1 could inhibit both wild-type and drug-resistant P. falciparum strains with IC50 5â7 nM albeit with relative less selectivity (Table 1). Nevertheless, we still considered 1 as a promising antimalarial lead compound with attractive development prospect.(28) Since our group has been devoted to research on the redevelopment of launched drugs or drug candidates,(29â33) in this paper, drug redevelopment research has been systematically conducted by treating 1 as a lead compound to further improve its antimalarial effect, reduce toxicity, and discover new chemical entities with new structure prototypes. Therefore, 31 novel hydroxamic acid derivatives (2â32) were designed and synthesized, and their antimalarial activities and cytotoxicity were systematically evaluated. Among them, compound 11 showed potency against several multiresistant malarial parasites in vitro, including two artemisinin-resistant clinical isolates, attenuated cytotoxicity, and both erythrocytic and liver-stage therapeutic effects in vivo with acceptable animal safety. Compound 11 displayed improved stability toward mouse liver microsomes, and the pharmacokinetic properties of 11 were also investigated. Western blot analysis showed that 11 could inhibit the function of PfHDAC, and we further identified PfHDAC1 as the target of 11 via PfHDAC1/2 gene knockdown experiments. Hence, 11 was identified in our research as a prospective prototype for innovation in malaria treatment.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Design and Synthesis of Derivatives

    Similar to other HDAC inhibitors,(8)1 consists of three components as follows: a hydroxamic acid group serving as a zinc-binding group (ZBG) that chelates the Zn2+ cofactor in the catalytic pocket, an N-methylindole fragment (CAP region) that recognizes and interacts with the amino acid residues at the entrance of the catalytic pocket, and a pyrimidinyl 4-aminomethyl piperidine fragment (linker) that connects the ZBG and CAP groups. Since hydroxamic acid and indole are critical pharmacophores and pyrimidinyl can improve hydrophilicity and synthetic feasibility, these structural domains were reserved in the preliminary stage of the structure design. Since the flexible aliphatic diamine linkers have been widely applied into HDAC inhibitiors,(34â38) a strategy of introducing the rigid spirocyclic linker was envisaged by us.(39â42) This strategy not only brought novel antimalarial chemical prototype but may also influence the biological properties of new compounds and reduce the cytotoxicity. On the other hand, replacement of the CAP group could also adjust the antimalarial potencies and safety of the newly designed derivatives (Scheme 1).
    Scheme 2 depicts the synthesis of spirocyclic derivatives 2â32. This procedure was modified from the reported synthetic route of 1.(43) Briefly, tert-butyl 2,8-diazaspiro[4.5]decane-2-carboxylate (33) coupled with ethyl 2-chloropyrimidine-5-carboxylate under alkaline condition to produce 34. Then, the tert-butoxycarbonyl (Boc) protecting group in 34 was removed in hydrogen chloride solution, and the resultant 35 was converted into 36 via benzyloxycarbonyl (Cbz) protection of the free amine group. Hydrolysis of ethyl ester produced acid 37, which was converted into 38 via condensation with O-(tetrahydro-2H-pyran-2-yl)hydroxylamine. Hydrogenolysis of the Cbz group produced 39, and compounds 2â32 were finally obtained by reductive amination and deprotection of the 2-tetrahydropyranyl (THP) group in hydrogen chloride.

    In Vitro Antiplasmodium Activity and Cytotoxicity of Compounds 1â32

    To preliminarily estimate their antiplasmodium potency, all derivatives were assayed for inhibitory activities against two P. falciparum parasites (3D7 and Dd2). The cytotoxicity of each derivative was also investigated in two human cell lines (HepG2 and 293T). For each compound, the potency against wild-type parasites (3D7) and chloroquine-resistant parasites (Dd2) was similar, signifying that cross resistance was absent. Encouragingly, 2 displayed slightly improved antiplasmodium activities and significantly attenuated cytotoxicity compared with 1, implying that linker modification was tolerant and could reduce cytotoxicity (Table 1).
    Subsequently, the N-methyl-3-indolyl group in the CAP region was replaced by other ring systems (Table 2). Notably, all derivatives displayed potent antiplasmodium activities (< 50 nM) regardless of different CAP groups. Considering the relative disadvantages of monocyclic and excessively large CAP groups, we systematically investigated various aromatic bicyclic CAP groups. To our delight, two naphthyl derivatives 20 and 26 displayed similar potency and selectivity compared to 2. Further screening of indolyl-shaped CAP groups revealed that several indolyl (11) and azaindolyl (16â19) groups could improve selectivity while scarcely weaken the potency.
    Table 2. In Vitro Antiplasmodium Activity and Cytotoxicity of Compounds 2â32a
    a

    Results are the mean Â± SD obtained from two independent biological repeats, DHA = dihydroartemisinin, ND = not determined.

    b

    Selectivity index for human cells is defined as IC50 (human cell)/IC50 (3D7).

    SAR of Compounds 1â32

    Preliminary data of bioassay in vitro revealed some noteworthy structureâactivity relationship (SAR) characteristics compounds 1â32 as follows: (1) the conformation and flexibility of linker can be regulated subtly, and the fixation strategy via spirocycle formation can be tolerated (2vs1); (2) modification of the substituents in the CAP region can regulate the antiplasmodium activity and cytotoxicity; (3) monocyclic (3â10), tricyclic, and tetracyclic (29â32) CAP groups are not beneficial for antiplasmodium activity; (4) when the CAP region is a naphthyl, quinolyl or isoquinolyl group, 2-naphthyl, and its nitrogen-containing ring analogues are slightly more potent than 1-naphthyl and corresponding nitrogen-containing ring analogues in antiplasmodium activity, but all are weaker than 2 (20â28); (5) when the CAP region is an indolyl-shaped group, replacing the nitrogen atom by oxygen or sulfur atoms can undermine antiplasmodium potency, and the simple removal of N-methyl in the indolyl can alleviate the acute cytotoxicity (2vs11â13); (6) generally, when the CAP region is one of various electron-deficient aromatic rings, the cytotoxicity of the compounds is attenuated; however, their antiplasmodium activity can be compromised, and only azaindolyl derivatives 16â19 have similar potency to 1. Based on these results, 2, indolyl derivative 11 and azaindolyl derivatives 16â19 were selected for further investigation of whether our modifications were reasonable.

    In Vitro Antiplasmodium Activity of Compounds 1, 2, 11, and 16â19 against Clinical Multidrug-Resistant Parasites

    To further investigate the ability to adapt these new derivatives for application, we evaluated the antiplasmodium activity of compounds 1, 2, 11, and 16â19 against five clinical P. falciparum isolates carrying various phenotypes of resistance against mainstream antimalarial drugs (Table 3), including two artemisinin-resistant parasites (6218 and 6320).(44,45) In this 72 h assay, for each compound, the differences in IC50 values between multidrug-resistant parasites and 3D7 parasites (Tables 1 and 2) were not significant, which implied the absence of cross resistance between the tested compounds and clinical antimalarial drugs. Remarkably, all compounds displayed low nanoscale IC50 values similar to DHA. Therefore, compounds 1, 2, 11, and 16â19 have the potential to resolve the problem of antimalarial drug resistance.

    In Vitro Metabolic Stability of Compounds 1, 2, 11, and 16â19

    Before the therapeutic assay in vivo, we preliminarily compared the metabolic stability of compounds 1, 2, 11, and 16â19. The metabolic stability was tested via a mouse liver microsome assay in vitro (Table 4). While the stability of 2 was similar to that of 1, the half-life of 11 was 4 times longer than that of 1. Remarkably, the half-life of azaindolyl derivatives 16â19 was even longer, which might result from the resistance of electron-deficient azaindolyl toward metabolic enzymes.

    In Vivo Erythrocytic Anti-Malarial Activity of Compounds 1, 2, 11, and 16â19

    Blood-stage antimalarial activity in vivo was tested via a P. yoelli-balb/c malaria model. Parasites were injected intraperitoneally into each mouse 24 h before treatment. Compounds were delivered once a day intraperitoneally for 5 consecutive days. Piperaquine phosphate (PPQ) was selected as a positive control. In the first 20 day experiment, 11 and 17 were compared with 1 at doses of 10 and 30 mg/kg (Figure 2A,B). One mouse died in each 1-treated group (10 and 30 mg/kg dose groups) on day 6, and no deaths occurred in the 11-treated and 17-treated groups, indicating that the newly designed derivatives 11 and 17 showed better safety than the lead compound 1. Although no parasitemia was observed on day 20 in the surviving mice, they all suffered from recrudescence after treatment. The intensity of recrudescence increased in the order 1 > 17 > 11. Therefore, 11 was selected for further experiments.
    Since recrudescence hinted at the need for a higher dosage, in the second experiment, the dose was increased to 60 mg/kg, and the period of observation was extended to 30 days to confirm the therapeutic effect (Figure 2C,D). Comparison of the mouse survival ratios of 1 and 2 revealed that simply replacing the diamine group could reduce animal toxicity. Compounds 2 and 18 were not totally tolerated, as one mouse in each group died after treatment. Although 16 and 19 manifested less toxicity, they could not prevent severe recrudescence. Only 11 achieved a balance between curative effect and toxicity, as all the treated mice survived, and no parasitemia was observed on day 30. In summary, 11 was better than both 1 and other derivatives in the balance of in vivo therapeutic effects and safety properties and was selected for further study.

    In Vivo Liver-Stage Antimalarial Activity of 1 and 11

    Before invading RBCs, malarial parasites develop asymptomatically in hepatocytes.(46) The elimination of liver-stage parasites will prevent paroxysm and the other devastating consequences of malaria. Therefore, 1 and 11 were tested against rodent P. berghei ANKA parasites (Figure 3).(47) Each mouse was infected with 5000 sporozoites followed by the simultaneous intravenous administration of the test compound at a single dose of 30 mg/kg. KDU691(48) was used as a positive control. Bioluminescence assays showed that after parasite inoculation, the blank group developed distinct liver-stage infection in the first 48 h followed by the systemic spread of erythrocytic development. At a single dose of 30 mg/kg, 1 was still not tolerated (three mice died in 2 h after compound administration) and could not wholly eradicate liver-stage infection since minor systemic spread was observed in one surviving mouse. However, the 11-treated groups showed neither disease development nor mouse death until 96 h after inoculation. Therefore, 11 could prevent liver-stage disease development with acceptable safety.

    Erythrocytic Stage-Specific Parasite Inhibition by 1 and 11

    Malarial parasites undergo complicated life stages and morphogenesis events during blood-stage development. To further investigate their erythrocytic parasite-killing properties, synchronized 3D7 parasites were treated with 1 or 11 at a 10 Ã IC50 (3D7) concentration in three indicated 12 h periods corresponding to three stages of the parasite life cycle (ring, trophozoite, and schizont). After treatment, the residual compound was removed by washing three times with the fresh culture medium, and parasites were cultivated in the compound-free medium for further observation (Figure 4A). At 50 h, parasites treated with compounds 1 or 11 could not develop normally into a new ring stage on morphological analysis (Figure 4B). Although the occurrence of parasitemia was similar in all treated groups at 50 h, after a further 96 h cultivation, the parasites treated with drugs in the schizont stage group showed the lowest parasitemia growth (Figure 4D). Therefore, although they could eliminate all-stage parasites, 1 and 11 displayed preferable antiplasmodium potency against schizonts, which might interdict schizont growth or reinvasion of late-stage erythrocytic parasites.

    RSA Assay of Compounds 1 and 11 against Artemisinin-Resistant Parasites

    Artemisinin-resistant parasites 6218 and 6320 mainly displayed drug resistance in the early ring stage, whereas it could not be directly detected during the 72 h assay (Table 3). Clinical ring-stage survival assays (RSAs) are the standard process to identify artemisinin drug resistance.(49) Therefore, we chose compounds 1 and 11 to evaluate their ârealâ antimalarial potency against artemisinin-resistant parasites via RSA. Encouragingly, while DHA failed to completely clear 6218 and 6320, both 1 and 11 greatly reduced the ring-stage parasite burden, attenuating the risk of incomplete treatment (Figure 5). Ring-stage 3D7 parasites, used as a control, were efficiently eliminated by DHA, 1, and 11. These results collectively demonstrated the medical potential of 11 to overcome the threat of ART-resistant parasites.

    Target Elucidation of Compounds 1 and 11

    Since 1 was previously identified as a potent class I and II-type human HDAC inhibitor,(50) we speculated that 1 and 11 might also inhibit the function of PfHDACs. Encouragingly, western blot analysis clearly displayed the hyperacetylation of parasite histone after treatment with a 20 Ã IC50 concentration of compound 1 or 11 (Figure 6 and Figure S4 in the Supporting Information), suggesting that 1 and 11 could interfere with the function of PfHDACs.
    Considering the homology between PfHDAC1 and human HDACs,(8) we were further interested in whether PfHDAC1 was the target of 1 and 11. To evaluate the inhibitory effect of compounds against PfHDACs, a glmS ribozyme system was introduced at the 3â²-terminus of the PfHDAC1 gene to generate 3D7HDAC1-glmS parasites.(51,52) This ribozyme will autocleave the corresponding mRNA once exogenous 2-amino-2-deoxy-D-glucose (GlcN) is added, resulting in posttranscriptionally downregulated expression of PfHDAC1. As a control, an epitope TY1 that is unable to degrade mRNA was substituted for the glmS sequence, generating 3D7HDAC1-TY1 parasites. Considering the relevance between PfHDAC1 and other class II HDACs, PfHDAC2 was also downregulated via this method (Figure S2A in the Supporting Information). The genetic modifications were examined by PCR analysis (Figure S2B-E in the Supporting Information), and RT-qPCR analysis further confirmed that the mRNA of PfHDAC1 or PfHDAC2 genes was reduced in 3D7HDAC1-glmS or 3D7HDAC2-glmS, respectively, upon the exogenous introduction of GlcN, which was absent in TY1-tagged parasites (Figure 7A,D). The down-regulation of PfHDAC1 was further demonstrated by western blot examination (Figure S3 in the Supporting Information). Importantly, while the GlcN-induced gene downregulation of 3D7HDAC2-glmS was unable to significantly alter the antimalarial IC50 values of compounds 1 and 11 (Figure 7EâG, P = 0.16 and 0.1189, respectively), the downregulation of 3D7HDAC1-glmS induced hypersensitivity of the parasites toward 1 and 11 treatment (Figure 7B,C,G, P < 0.001 and = 0.0014, respectively). These results indirectly demonstrated that PfHDAC1 is an antimalarial molecular target of compounds 1 and 11. Currently, among three Zn2+-containing PfHDACs, only PfHDAC1 has been produced as a recombinant protein. Although the purification and activity of protein samples have been doubted,(51) we still performed the inhibitory study of 1 and 11 against recombinant PfHDAC1. Fortunately, both two compounds significantly suppressed the function of PfHDAC1 with low nanoscale IC50 values (Figure 7G). These results further justify that 1 and 11 were PfHDAC1 inhibitors.

    Inhibition of Compounds 1 and 11 against Human HDACs

    In previous anticancer research, 1 significantly suppressed the functions of human HDACs.(51) Considering the structural similarity between 1 and 11, in this section, we also compared the inhibitory effects of compounds 1 and 11 against six recombinant human HDACs (Table 5). These HDACs contain class I (hHDAC1 â¼3 and 8), class II (hHDAC6) and class III (hSirt2) HDACs. Similar to previous report, 1 significantly suppressed the enzymatic activities of all class I hHDACs with IC50 values lower than 10 nM and inhibited hHDAC6 with slightly reduced potency. Unfortunately, 11 was merely improved in hHDAC inhibition compared to 1. Hence, in the future modifications, we would focus on reducing this adverse inhibition of human HDACs. Although 11 was similar to 1 in inhibiting human HDACs, in the cell and animal experiments, the toxicity of compound 11 is significantly lower than that of compound 1. These results might imply that the cause of compound 1âs high toxicity is at least not only due to its inhibitory activity on human HDACs. In addition, both 1 and 11 did not interfere with hSirt2 since the absence of the Zn2+ cation in this enzyme.

    Mouse Pharmacokinetic Parameters of Compounds 1 and 11In Vivo

    As compound 11 showed potent therapeutic effects in a mouse model, we primarily compared its mouse pharmacokinetic (PK) properties in vivo with those of 1 (Table 6). Female BALB/c mice were administered a single dose of the tested compound intravenously (2 mg/kg) intraperitoneally (5 mg/kg) or orally (10 mg/kg). After intravenous administration, 11 had a Cmax of 260 ng/mL and a T1/2 of 0.72 h. Although the intraperitoneal half-life and AUC value of 11 were lower than 1, the Cmax of 11 was higher than 1, which might account for the improved erythrocytic therapeutic effect of 11in vivo. However, for compound 11, the oral Cmax was significantly lower than intraperitoneal value, and the oral bioavailability was only 0.5%. These results justified the reasonability of intraperitoneal administration of 11 in animal experiments.

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The crisis of multidrug resistance calls for the discovery of next-generation antimalarial drugs. In this study, structurally novel PfHDAC inhibitors with enhanced antimalarial activity were discovered via chemical modification. Based on clinical anticancer candidate 1, 31 completely new spirocyclic pyrimidinyl hydroxamic acid derivatives have been designed, synthesized, and tested for antimalarial potency and cytotoxicity, among which five derivatives, 2, 11, and 16â19, were found to inherit the nanoscale IC50 values of 1 against drug-sensitive and chloroquine-resistant P. falciparum parasites, while their cytotoxicity was attenuated 3â10 times compared to that of 1. A preliminary SAR study showed that the fixation of central diamine via spirocycle formation led to reduced cytotoxicity, and appropriate structural optimizations of the CAP group could further weaken cytotoxicity while preserving antimalarial potency.
    Compounds 11 and 16â19 could also eliminate several multidrug-resistant P. falciparum clinical isolates, and they also had enhanced metabolic stability against mouse liver microsomes in vitro compared to 1. However, only 11 effectively cured blood-stage parasites with sufficient safety margins in vivo at 60 mg/kg. Compound 11 could also prevent asymptomatic liver-stage infection from invading RBCs in vivo. Western blot analysis clearly showed that 11 eliminated parasites by inhibiting the function of PfHDACs. PfHDAC1/2 gene downregulation and in vitro inhibition of recombinant PfHDAC1 experiments further confirmed 11 as a novel PfHDAC1 inhibitor. Although 11 still significantly inhibited several human HDACs, considering that only a few PfHDAC1 inhibitors have been reported thus far, 11 will not only be a new starting point for antimalarial drug development but also play a critical role in the chemobiological study of PfHDAC1 regulation in the survival, reproduction, and virulence of malarial parasites. Further structural optimization of 11 is currently in progress in our laboratory.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Chemical Synthesis and Analytical Data

    Sample 1 was purchased from WuXi AppTec. Unless otherwise noted, reagents and solvents were purchased from commercial suppliers and were directly used without further purification. Reaction conditions were not optimized. Analytical thin-layer chromatography (TLC) was performed using an HSGF 254 (150â200 Î¼m thickness; Yantai Huiyou Co., China), and spots were visualized with UV light, iodine, and potassium permanganate staining. Flash column chromatography was performed using an HSGF 254 (Yantai Huiyou Co., China). Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy was performed on a Bruker AMX-400 NMR and Ascend 600 MHz NMR (IS as TMS). Chemical shifts were reported in parts per million (ppm, Î´) downfield from tetramethylsilane. Proton coupling patterns were described as singlet (s), doublet (d), triplet (t), quartet (q), multiplet (m), and broad (br). High-resolution mass spectra (HRMS) were obtained by electrospray ionization (ESI) using a Waters GCT Premie and Waters LCT. Melting point analysis of solid compounds was performed on a SGW X-4 microscopic melting point apparatus equipped with a JM 628 digital thermometer, and the melting point was not corrected. HPLC analysis of final compounds was performed on an Agilent 1100 with a quaternary pump and diode-array detector (DAD). The HPLC peak purity of final compounds was verified by UV spectra, and all compounds were confirmed to be â¥95% pure. All derivatives were identified with non-PAINS on the web at http://zinc15.docking.org/patterns/home/ recommended by editors from the ACS (American Chemical Society).

    Synthesis of tert-Butyl 8-(5-(Ethoxycarbonyl)pyrimidine-2-yl)-2,8-diazaspiro[4.5]decane-2-carboxylate (34)

    Compound 33 (24 g, 100 mmol) and ethyl 2-chloropyrimidine-5-carboxylate (22.4 g, 120 mmol) were dissolved in CH2Cl2 (500 mL) under nitrogen protection. The solution was cooled to 0â5 Â°C, and iPr2NEt (25 mL, 151 mmol) was added in one portion. The mixture was then stirred at 25 Â°C until TLC (PE/EA = 3/1) implied the completion of the reaction (ca. 6 h). The mixture was washed with water (500 mL) and brine (500 mL), and the organic phase was evaporated in vacuo to dryness. The residue was purified by flash column chromatography (SiO2, PE/EA) to obtain compound 34 (33.7 g, 86% yield). mp 108â110 Â°C (CH3OH/CH2Cl2); 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) Î´ 8.77 (s, 2H), 4.26 (q, J = 7.1 Hz, 2H), 3.93 (m, 2H), 3.81 (m, 2H), 3.31 (m, 2H), 3.15 (m, 2H), 1.77 (m, 2H), 1.52 (m, 4H), 1.40 (s, 9H), 1.29 (t, J = 7.1 Hz, 3H).

    Synthesis of Ethyl 2-(2,8-Diazaspiro[4.5]decan-8-yl)pyrimidine-5-carboxylate (35)

    Compound 34 (39 g, 100 mmol) was dissolved in CH2Cl2 (500 mL), and HCl/1,4-dioxane solution (4 M, 100 mL, 400 mmol) was added gradually in one portion. The mixture was stirred at 25 Â°C for 6 h; during that time, a white solid precipitated from the solution. After TLC (PE/EA = 2/1) showed that the reaction was complete, the mixture was evaporated in vacuo to dryness. The resultant oily crude 35 product was directly used without further purification. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) Î´ 8.77 (s, 2H), 4.26 (q, J = 7.1 Hz, 2H), 3.90 (m, 2H), 3.78 (m, 2H), 3.24 (m, 2H), 3.10 (m, 2H), 1.77 (m, 2H), 1.52 (m, 4H), 1.29 (t, J = 7.1 Hz, 3H).

    Synthesis of Benzyl 8-(5-(Ethoxycarbonyl)pyrimidine-2-yl)-2,8-diazaspiro[4.5]decane-2-carboxylate (36)

    To above-obtained oily crude 36 (synthesized from 39 g of 34) was added CH2Cl2 (500 mL), and the mixture was stirred under nitrogen protection until the oil completely dissolved.     Synthesis of 2-(2-((Benzyloxy)carbonyl)-2,8-diazaspiro[4.5]decan-8-yl)pyrimidine-5-carboxylic Acid (37)

    To a mixture of 36 (40 g, 94 mmol) and potassium carbonate (35 g, 253 mmol) were added 250 mL of distilled water and 250 mL of CH3OH.     Synthesis of Benzyl 8-(5-(((Tetrahydro-2H-pyran-2-yl)oxy)carbamoyl)pyrimidine-2-yl)-2,8-diazaspiro[4.5]decane-2-carboxylate (38)

    Solid crude 37 (synthesized from 40 g 36) was dissolved in 235 mL of N,N-dimethylformamide and 235 mL of CH2Cl2 under nitrogen protection, and HOBt (25.4 g, 188 mmol) and EDCI (36 g, 188 mmol) were added in turn. After the mixture was stirred for 30 min, THPONH2 (33.5 g, 286 mmol), and Et3N (40 mL, 288 mmol) were added in turn. The mixture was further stirred for 48 h. After TLC (CH2Cl2/CH3OH = 9/1) showed that the reaction was complete, the mixture was poured into saturated aqueous sodium bicarbonate solution. The organic phase was first washed with a large amount of water, shaken twice with brine (200 mL), and then dried with anhydrous sodium sulfate. The solvent was evaporated in vacuo, and the residue was purified by flash column chromatography (SiO2, PE/EA) to obtain compound 38 (38.5 g, 82% yield). mp 182â184 Â°C (CH3OH/CH2Cl2); 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) Î´ 11.51 (s, 1H), 8.67 (s, 2H), 7.46â7.28 (m, 5H), 5.07 (s, 2H), 4.95 (m, 1H), 4.09â3.99 (m, 1H), 3.96â3.72 (m, 4H), 3.53 (m, 1H), 3.41 (m, 2H), 3.25 (m, 2H), 1.80 (m, 2H), 1.70 (m, 3H), 1.53 (m, 7H).

    Synthesis of 2-(2,8-Diazaspiro[4.5]decan-8-yl)-N-((tetrahydro-2H-pyran-2-yl)oxy)pyrimidine-5-carboxamide (39)

    Compound 38 (0.5 g 1 mmol) was dissolved in 2.5 mL of CH2Cl2 and 2.5 mL of CH3OH, and 10% Pd/C (0.21 g, wetted with ca. 55% water) and Et3SiH (0.8 mL, 5 mmol) were added under nitrogen protection. The solvent was evaporated to dryness in a rotary evaporator, and the residue was purified by column chromatography (SiO2, CH2Cl2/CH3OH) to obtain compound 39. mp 156â158 Â°C (CH3OH/CH2Cl2); 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-d6) Î´ 8.69 (s, 2H), 4.95 (m, 1H), 4.02 (m, 1H), 3.82 (m, 4H), 3.50 (m, 1H), 3.20 (m, 2H), 2.98 (s, 2H), 1.80 (m, 2H), 1.69 (m, 3H), 1.54 (m, 7H).

    General Synthesis of Compounds 2â32

    To compound 39 (0.36 g, 1 mmol) was added CH3OH (10 mL), corresponding R-CHO (1.2 mmol), and NaBH3CN (0.31 g, 5 mmol), and the mixture stirred at 25 Â°C for 24 h. The reaction mixture was then poured into saturated aqueous sodium bicarbonate solution, and the aqueous phase was extracted three times with CH2Cl2 (25 mL). The combined organic layers were washed once with brine (100 mL) and dried with anhydrous sodium sulfate, and the solvent was removed in vacuo. The residue was purified by flash column chromatography (SiO2, CH2Cl2/CH3OH), and the product was dissolved in CH2Cl2 (0.05 M) under a nitrogen atmosphere. HCl/1,4-dioxane (4 M, 2.4 equiv) was then added in one portion. After stirring at 25 Â°C for 30 min, the mixture was filtered, and the filtrate was washed with a large amount of CH2Cl2 and dried in vacuo to obtain the compound.

    Parasite Culture and Transfection

    P. falciparum was cultured based on a standard protocol.(53) For simplification, all parasites were cultivated in an atmosphere consisting of 5% CO2, 5% O2, and 90% N2 with a completed medium (CM) consisting of RPMI (10.44 g/L), HEPES (5.94 g/L), Albumax (5 g/L), and sodium bicarbonate (2.2 g/L) and supplemented with hypoxanthine (50 mg/L) and gentamycin (50 mg/L). Fifty micrograms of the plasmid was used for electroporation transfection in the ring phase of 7% parasitemia as reported previously.(52) Transfected parasites were screened with 5 nM WR99210 (Jacobus Pharmaceuticals) or 2.5 Î¼g/mL blasticidin-S deaminase (Life Technologies), and the clones were diluted to specified concentrations. Parasite genomic DNAs were extracted for verification of gene editing by PCRR.(54)

    In Vitro Antiplasmodium Activity Assay

    A 72 h parasite growth inhibitory assay was performed as described previously.(55) Briefly, highly synchronized ring-stage parasites (1% parasitemia and 2% hematocrit) were cultured with the compound of interest in 96-well plates of 200 Î¼L per well. The compound was diluted serially from an initial concentration of 200 nM. The parasites were cultured for 72 h followed by lysis as described above. The inhibition of each compound was normalized to the DMSO negative control, and IC50 values were calculated by GraphPad Prism 7.

    In Vitro Cytotoxicity Assay

    Mammalian cell lines HepG2 and 293T were cultured in 10 cm dishes with Dulbeccoâs modified Eagle medium (DMEM) consisting of 10% fetal bovine serum (FBS) and 1% penicillin/streptomycin (P/S) at 37 Â°C in an atmosphere consisting of 5% CO2. For the cytotoxicity assay, 10,000 cells were seeded into each well of a white 96-well plate, and the total volume was 100 Î¼L. After 24 h, the medium was replaced with the new medium containing the test compounds. The compound was diluted serially from an initial concentration of 100 Î¼M. After 72 h of cultivation, a cell viability assay was performed on CellTiter-Glo (Promega cat. no. G7572). The inhibition of each compound was normalized to that of the DMSO negative control, and IC50 values were calculated by GraphPad Prism 7.

    Liver Microsome Metabolism Assay

    Liver microsomes (0.5 mg/mL) were purchased from Corning Corporation. Ketanserin was used as a positive control. The test compounds were first dissolved in DMSO to a concentration of 10 mM and then diluted to 0.5 mM with acetonitrile. The compound was diluted to a working concentration of 1.5 Î¼M using liver microsome buffer, and then 30 Î¼L of the above mixture was mixed with 15 Î¼L of 6 mM NADPH at 37 Â°C. At 0, 5, 15, 30, and 45 min after incubation, 135 Î¼L of acetonitrile was added to extinguish the reaction. The mixture was shaken on a shaker (IKA, MTS 2/4) at 600 rpm for 10 min and then centrifuged at 5600Ãg for 15 min (Thermo MultifugeÃ3R). The supernatant was diluted to distilled water by 1:1 and analyzed by LCâMS/MS.

    Preparation of Test Compound Solutions

    The solvents were DMSO (A) and 20 wt % aqueous 2-hydroxypropyl-Î²-cyclodextrin solution (B). The compounds were first dissolved in 5 v/v% A and then diluted with 95 v/v% B to the indicated concentration. Unless otherwise noted, in animal experiments, the injected volume was 15 Î¼L/g based on the average body weight of the mice in each group (see Figure S1 in the Supporting Information), and the concentration was calculated accordingly. The solution was freshly prepared before administration.

    In Vivo Erythrocytic Antimalarial Assay

    All animal experimental procedures followed the National Institutes of Health Guide for the Care and Use of Laboratory Animals under the supervision of Animal Welfare and Committee of Institute Pasteur of shanghai, Chinese Academy of Sciences (IACUC issue no. A2018009). An in vivo erythrocytic antimalarial assay was conducted in a female Balb/c mouse-P. yoelii rodent malaria model. Each group contained five mice. On day 0, each mouse (6â8 weeks old) was inoculated with 105 parasites via intraperitoneal injection (i.p.).(56) After 24 h, the solution of compounds or solvent (for the blank group) was delivered at the indicated dose via i.p. once daily for 5 successive days. Piperaquine phosphate (PPQ) was used as a positive control. Tail vein blood smears were collected on the indicated days, and the percentage of parasitemia was determined via microscopic examination of 5000 RBCs and expressed as the mean Â± SD.

    In Vivo Liver-Stage Antimalarial Assay

    An in vivo liver-stage antimalarial assay was conducted in a female Balb/c mouse-P. berghei ANKA rodent malaria parasite model. Each group contained five female Balb/c mice (6â8 weeks old). On day 0, each mouse was inoculated with 5000 P. berghei-ANKA-GFP-luc sporozoites via intravenous injection (i.v.) after which the solution of test compound or the solvent (for the blank group) was delivered at a single indicated dose via i.v. (day 0). KDU691 was used as a positive control. Three milligrams of d-luciferin sodium (SinoChrom BC-220-10) in 100 Î¼L of ddH2O was administered to each mouse via intraperitoneal injection for parasite fluorescence detection. The fluorescence signals were visualized by IVIS Lumina series and detected on days 1â4 after infection.

    Erythrocytic Stage-Specific Parasite Inhibition Assay

    To analyze stage-specific parasite inhibition, highly synchronized 3D7 parasites were cultured in 24-well plates with an initial parasitemia of 1% and hematocrit of 2%. After incubation with the test compound at a concentration of 10 Ã IC50 (3D7) for the indicated 12 h, the parasites were washed three times to remove the compound and diluted with CM to a ratio of 1:40. DMSO was used as a blank control. Morphological analysis of parasites was conducted by microscopic examination of a Giemsa-staining smear. Parasitemia was counted by flow cytometry (MoFlo Astrios EQ; BECKMAN COULTER).

    RSA Assay

    A ring-stage survival assay (RSA) was performed to evaluate the potency of compounds against artemisinin-resistant P. falciparum clinical isolates 6218 and 6320 carrying kelch 13 mutation C580Y as described previously.(49) Briefly, parasites were treated with 5% d-sorbitol after being highly synchronized at 0â3 h post invasion by 60%/40% Percoll gradient separation. Parasite mixed liquor (0.5â1% parasitemia, hematocrit 2%) was seeded into each well of the 48-well plate with the compound treatment at a final concentration of 700 nM for 6 h. Parasites were cultured for 66 h with CM after three washes with 15 mL of CM each time. Parasitemia was calculated for at least 10,000 RBCs from a blood smear. The survival rate of the DMSO control was counted to calculate the percentage. Dihydroartemisinin (DHA) was used as a positive control.

    Flow Cytometry

    Flow cytometry was performed as previously described.(57) Briefly, RBCs infected with parasites were fixed in 4% formaldehyde/0.015% glutaraldehyde for 30 min at room temperature and then mixed with 500 Î¼L of SYBR green staining solution (Invitrogen) (1:5000) in 1 Ã PBS (phosphate-buffered in saline, pH 7.4, free of calcium and magnesium) solution. Prior to flow cytometry analysis on the MoFlo Astrios EQ instrument (BECKMAN COULTER), the cells were washed three times with 1 Ã PBS. RBCs (excitation 488 nm; emission detection FL1 513 Â± 26 nm, 5 Ã 105) were measured, and the 5 (SYBR green intensity) value on FL1 was used as a threshold to identify (gated) infected RBCs. FlowJo software (v.10.0.5) was used to process the acquired data.

    Plasmid Construction for Conditional Knock-Down of PfHDAC1/2

    The gene conditional knock-down strategy was based on the glmS system.(51) The glmS sequence was inserted into the C-terminus of the targeted genes by using the CRISPR/Cas9 system.(52) Briefly, the C-terminus of PfHDAC1 or PfHDAC2 was fused with 3 Ã TY1 or 3 Ã TY1 plus glmS. Primers 1/2 and 3/4 and primers 5/6 and 7/8 were used to amplify the homologous arms of PfHDAC1 and PfHDAC2, respectively. Primers 9/10 and 11/12 were used to amplify the 3 Ã TY1 tag and glmS sequences for PfHDAC2 editing and primers 13/14 and 15/16 for PfHDAC1, respectively. Primers 1/4 for PfHDAC2 and primers 5/8 for PfHDAC1 were used for overlap PCR to get the full donor sequences. All molecular clones were completed by the ClonExpress II One Step Cloning Kit (Vazyme C112-02). The purified PCR products were inserted into pL6CS at the ASC I/Afl II sites. The sgRNA (single guide RNA) was made by annealing primers 21/22 with its antisense strand for PfHDAC1 and PfHDAC2, respectively, and was inserted into pL6CS at the Xho I/Avr II sites. The successful plasmids were used to do the Parasite transfections, which was performed by electroporation at the ring stage of 7% parasitemia by using 50 Î¼g of the plasmid as reported previously. Transfected parasites were selected by blasticidin-S deaminase (Life Technologies) at 2.5 Î¼g/mL or 5 nM WR99210 (Jacobus Pharmaceuticals) and cloned by limiting dilution. Parasite genomic DNAs were extracted for validation of gene-editing by PCR. Gene knock-in was validated by PCR using primers 17/18 and 19/20, followed by DNA sequencing. The schematic explanation, PCR, and western blot identification of the strategy is displayed in Figures S2 and S3 in the Supporting Information.

    Western Blot Assay

    For identification of parasite histone hyperacetylation: Parasite samples were first treated with 0.15% saponin to remove the RBC proteins and then sonicated in 1% NP40 separation buffer. After centrifugation at 12,000Ãg for 15 min, the supernatant was resuspended in conventional SDS loading buffer. Parasite proteins were separated by 10% SDS-PAGE and transferred onto PVDF membranes (Millipore). Immunodetection was performed using anti-histone H3 antibody (ABclonal, A2348) or anti-histone H3 acetylated antibody (Millipore, 06-599) and secondary antibody that binds to HRP (Jackson ImmunoResearch Laboratories). The chemiluminescent HRP substrate Immobilon Western Kit (Millipore) was used for signal detection.
    For identification of PfHDAC1 downregulation: The 3D7HDAC1-TY1 and 3D7HDAC1-glmS parasites in the schizont stage were cultivated for 48 h with 2.5 mM glucosamine (GlcN). The parasite samples were collected and first treated with 0.15% saponin followed by sonicating in 1% NP40 separation buffer. After centrifugation at 12,000Ãg for 15 min, the supernatant was resuspended in conventional SDS loading buffer. Parasite proteins were separated by 10% SDS-PAGE and transferred onto PVDF membranes (Millipore). Immunodetection was performed using anti-TY1 antibody (diagenode, c15200054) and secondary antibody that binds to HRP (Jackson ImmunoResearch Laboratories). The chemiluminescent HRP substrate Immobilon Western Kit (Millipore) was used for signal detection. Actin was used as loading control.

    RT-qPCR Assay

    Parasite total RNA was extracted according to the manufacturerâs instructions for the TRIzol Kit (Life Technologies). According to the product manual, the complementary DNAs were synthesized using a HiScript II Q Select RT SuperMix Kit for qPCR (Vazyme, R223-01). QPCR was performed on the ABI 7900 system with the following procedure: initial denaturation was performed at 95 Â°C for 5 min, and then 40 cycles of 10 s were performed at 95 Â°C, 50 Â°C for 20 s, and 60 Â°C for 30 s. The housekeeping gene seryl-tRNA synthetase (PF3D7_0717700) was used as an internal control. Primers used in PfHDAC1/2 gene knock-down study are listed in Table S2 in the Supporting Information.

    In Vitro Sensitivity Assay of Compounds toward PfHDAC1/2 Knock-Down Parasites

    The assay was performed as described previously.(42) After highly synchronized, schizont-stage 3D7HDAC1/2-glmS or 3D7HDAC1/2-TY1 parasites were pretreated with 2.5 mM glucosamine (GlcN) until the next ring-stage to knockdown the expression of PfHDAC1/2, the parasites were then incubated in a 96-well plate (1% parasitemia, and 2% hematocrit) with compounds from an initial concentration of 1 Î¼M with a gradient dilution in the total volume of 200 Î¼L. After 4 h incubation, the parasites were washed three times by an in-completed medium to remove compounds and continued culture for 68 h before SYBR Green I-staining as described above. The IC50 values were calculated by GraphPad Prism 7.

    In Vitro Recombinant Human HDAC Inhibition Assay

    Human HDAC 1â3, 6, and 8 and Sirt 2 were purchased from BPS. SAHA and Suramin were selected as positive control. Tested compound were dissolved into 20 mM stock in 100% DMSO.
    HDAC 1â3 and 6 inhibition: In the 96-well plate, compound at test concentrations (10, 3.33, 1.11, 0.37, 0.12, 0.041, 0.014, 0.0046, 0.0015, 0.005 Î¼M) was incubated with enzyme/Tris buffer solution (15 Î¼L) at 25 Â°C for 15 min. To each well, trypsin and Ac-peptide substrate/Tris buffer solution (10 Î¼L) were added to start reaction. The plates were incubated at 25 Â°C for 60 min and then analyzed on Synergy MX (excitation 355 nm, emission 460 nm).
    HDAC 8 inhibition: In the 96-well plate, compound at test concentrations (10, 3.33, 1.11, 0.37, 0.12, 0.041, 0.014, 0.0046, 0.0015, 0.005 Î¼M) was incubated with enzyme/Tris buffer solution (15 Î¼L) at 25 Â°C for 15 min. To each well, Ac-peptide substrate/Tris buffer solution (10 Î¼L) was added to start reaction. The plates were incubated at 25 Â°C for 240 min; then trypsin solution was added and the incubation was continued for further 120 min. The plate was analyzed on Synergy MX (excitation 355 nm, emission 460 nm).
    Sirt 2 inhibition: In the 96-well plate, compound at test concentrations (10, 3.33, 1.11, 0.37, 0.12, 0.041, 0.014, 0.0046, 0.0015, 0.005 Î¼M) was incubated with enzyme/Tris buffer solution (15 Î¼L) at 25 Â°C for 15 min. To each well, Ac-peptide substrate/Tris buffer solution (10 Î¼L) was added to start reaction. The plates were incubated at 25 Â°C for 240 min, trypsin solution was added, and then the incubation was continued for further 120 min. The plate was analyzed on Synergy MX (excitation 355 nm, emission 460 nm).

    In Vitro Recombinant PfHDAC1 Inhibition Assay

    In vitro inhibition assay of PfHDAC1 sample was performed according to previous report.(58) The recombinant PfHDAC1 (BPS bioscience, 50063) and the HDAC-Glo I/II Assays and Screening System (Promega, G6431) were all commercially available. Briefly, in a 96-well plate, compounds 1 and 11 were diluted with a 3-fold gradient from an initial concentration of 50 and 200 Î¼M, respectively, to 14 wells in the total volume of 100 Î¼L in HDAC-Glo I/II Buffer. An amount of 200 ng of PfHDAC1 was then added to each well. After incubation for 1.5 h, 100 Î¼L of the HDAC-Glo I/II substrate with a developer regent was added to each well. After incubation 30 min, luminous values were detected by a microplate reader (bioteck). Positive control contained no compound, negative control contained no compound, and no PfHDAC1. IC50 was calculated by GraphPad Prism 7.

    Pharmacokinetic Assay

    Female Balb/c mice (18â19 g, 6â8 weeks) were purchased from Beijing Vital River Biotechnology Co., Ltd. All mice were kept at controlled temperature and humidity and were given access to sterilized water and diet ad libitum. Solutions of the test compounds were prepared at concentrations of 0.4 mg/mL for i.v. and 1 mg/mL for i.p. Each mouse received one compound solution at the indicated dose and mode of administration. At 0.25, 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 8, and 24 h after administration, blood samples were collected into K2EDTA-containing test tubes and centrifuged at 82,000 rpm for 6 min at 4 Â°C. LCâMS/MS analysis of plasma was performed on a Shimadzu LC30AD and API 5000 equipped with a Phenomenex Luna Omega C18 1.6 Î¼m column (2.1 Ã 50 mm). The mobile phase was a mixture of phase A (0.1% formic acid in water) and phase B (0.1% formic acid in acetonitrile), running at a flow rate gradient of 0.6 mL/min at 60 Â°C. The mass spectrum was obtained on an API 5000 triple quadrupole equipped with an ESI source. Propranolol was used as an internal standard. The plasma concentration of the compound was analyzed, and the pharmacokinetic parameters were calculated by WinNonlin.

    Statistical Analysis

    The results are shown as the mean Â± SEM from three independent experiments or the mean Â± SD from two independent experiments. Graphical representation and statistical analyses were conducted using GraphPad Prism 7 software. Differences were tested for statistical significance using two-tailed Studentâs t-test.

      Antimalarial drug discovery - the path towards eradication
      Burrows Jeremy N; Burlot Emilie; Campo Brice; Cherbuin Stephanie; Jeanneret Sarah; Leroy Didier; Spangenberg Thomas; Waterson David; Wells Timothy Nc; Willis Paul
      Parasitology (2014), 141 (1), 128-39 ISSN:.
      Malaria is a disease that still affects a significant proportion of the global human population. Whilst advances have been made in lowering the numbers of cases and deaths, it is clear that a strategy based solely on disease control year on year, without reducing transmission and ultimately eradicating the parasite, is unsustainable. This article highlights the current mainstay treatments alongside a selection of emerging new clinical molecules from the portfolio of Medicines for Malaria Venture (MMV) and our partners. In each case, the key highlights from each research phase are described to demonstrate how these new potential medicines were discovered. Given the increased focus of the community on eradicating the disease, the strategy for next generation combination medicines that will provide such potential is explained.

      Lysine Deacetylase Inhibitors in Parasites: Past, Present, and Future Perspectives
      Hailu, Gebremedhin S.; Robaa, Dina; Forgione, Mariantonietta; Sippl, Wolfgang; Rotili, Dante; Mai, Antonello
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2017), 60 (12), 4780-4804CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      Current therapies for human parasite infections rely on a few drugs, most of which have severe side effects, and their helpfulness is being seriously compromised by the drug resistance problem. Globally, this is pushing discovery research of antiparasitic drugs toward new agents endowed with new mechanisms of action. By using a "drug repurposing" strategy, histone deacetylase inhibitors (HDACi), which are presently clin. approved for cancer use, are now under investigation for various parasite infections. Because parasitic Zn2+- and NAD+-dependent HDACs play crucial roles in the modulation of parasite gene expression and many of them are pro-survival for several parasites under various conditions, they are now emerging as novel potential antiparasitic targets. This Perspective summarizes the state of knowledge of HDACi (both class I/II HDACi and sirtuin inhibitors) targeted to the main human parasitic diseases (schistosomiasis, malaria, trypanosomiasis, leishmaniasis, and toxoplasmosis) and provides visions into the main issues that challenge their development as antiparasitic agents.

      Differential association of Orc1 and Sir2 proteins to telomeric domains in Plasmodium falciparum
      Mancio-Silva, Liliana; Rojas-Meza, Ana Paola; Vargas, Miguel; Scherf, Artur; Hernandez-Rivas, Rosaura
      Journal of Cell Science (2008), 121 (12), 2046-2053CODEN: JNCSAI; ISSN:0021-9533. (Company of Biologists Ltd.)
      Telomeres have the capacity to recruit proteins that facilitate the spreading of heterochromatin into subtelomeric DNA regions. In the human protozoan pathogen Plasmodium falciparum, the telomere-assocd. protein Sir2 has been shown to control the silencing of members of virulence genes at some, but not all, chromosome-end loci, indicating that addnl. proteins are involved in telomere position effect. Here, we identified, in P. falciparum, a novel telomere-assocd. protein that displays homol. with the origin-of-recognition-complex 1 protein Orc1. Relocation of Orc1 and Sir2 was also linked to the partial dissocn. of telomere clusters. Super gel-shift and chromatin-immunopptn. expts. showed the phys. assocn. of Orc1 with telomere repeats but revealed a differential assocn. with adjacent non-coding repeat DNA elements. The data suggest that Plasmodium telomeres might fold back and that Orc1 cooperates with Sir2 in telomeric silencing.

      Comparative gene expression profiling of P. falciparum malaria parasites exposed to three different histone deacetylase inhibitors
      Andrews, Katherine T.; Gupta, Archna P.; Tran, Thanh N.; Fairlie, David P.; Gobert, Geoffrey N.; Bozdech, Zbynek
      PLoS One (2012), 7 (2), e31847CODEN: POLNCL; ISSN:1932-6203. (Public Library of Science)
      Histone deacetylase (HDAC) inhibitors are being intensively pursued as potential new drugs for a range of diseases, including malaria. HDAC inhibitors are also important tools for the study of epigenetic mechanisms, transcriptional control, and other important cellular processes. In this study the effects of three structurally related antimalarial HDAC inhibitors on P. falciparum malaria parasite gene expression were compared. The three hydroxamate-based compds., trichostatin A (TSA), suberoylanilide hydroxamic acid (SAHA; Vorinostat) and a 2-aminosuberic acid deriv. (2-ASA-9), all caused profound transcriptional effects, with â¼ 2-21% of genes having >2-fold altered expression following 2 h exposure to the compds. Only two genes, alpha tubulin II and a hydrolase, were up-regulated by all three compds. after 2 h exposure in all biol. replicates examd. The transcriptional changes obsd. after 2 h exposure to HDAC inhibitors were found to be largely transitory, with only 1-5 % of genes being regulated after removing the compds. and culturing for a further 2 h. Despite some structural similarity, the three inhibitors caused quite diverse transcriptional effects, possibly reflecting subtle differences in mode of action or cellular distribution. This dataset represents an important contribution to our understanding of how HDAC inhibitors act on malaria parasites and identifies alpha tubulin II as a potential transcriptional marker of HDAC inhibition in malaria parasites that may be able to be exploited for future development of HDAC inhibitors as new antimalarial agents.

      HDAC inhibitors in parasitic diseases

      A review. Parasitic diseases cause significant global morbidity and mortality, particularly in underdeveloped regions of the world.       Towards histone deacetylase inhibitors as new antimalarial drugs
      Andrews, Katherine T.; Tran, Thanh N.; Fairlie, David P.
      Current Pharmaceutical Design (2012), 18 (24), 3467-3479CODEN: CPDEFP; ISSN:1381-6128. (Bentham Science Publishers Ltd.)
      A review. Histone deacetylases (HDACs) are important enzymes that effect post-translational modifications of proteins by altering the acetylation state of lysine residues. HDACs control epigenetic changes that trigger cell transformation and proliferation of transformed cells assocd. with many diseases. These enzymes are validated drug targets for some types of cancer and are promising therapeutic targets for a range of other diseases, including malaria. Annually, there are â¼500 million clin. cases of malaria and â¼0.8-1.2 million deaths. There is no licensed vaccine for preventing malaria, and parasites that cause malaria are becoming resistant to current drugs, necessitating the search for new therapies. HDAC inhibitors are emerging as a promising new class of antimalarial drugs with potent and selective action against Plasmodium parasites in vitro. Recent studies on the effects of HDAC inhibitors on the growth and development of P. falciparum have provided important new information on transcriptional regulation in malaria parasites and have validated the potential of this class of inhibitors for malaria therapy. To realize effective HDAC inhibitors for clin. trials, next generation inhibitors must not inhibit other human HDACs or proteins required for normal human physiol., be highly selective in killing parasites in vivo without killing normal host cells, and have improved bioavailability and pharmacokinetic profiles. This review summarizes current knowledge about malaria parasite HDACs and HDAC inhibitors with antimalarial properties, and provides insights for their development into new drugs for treatment of malaria.

      Sir2a regulates rDNA transcription and multiplication rate in the human malaria parasite Plasmodium falciparum
      Mancio-Silva Liliana; Lopez-Rubio Jose Juan; Claes Aurelie; Scherf Artur
      Nature communications (2013), 4 (), 1530 ISSN:.
      The Plasmodium falciparum histone deacetylase Sir2a localizes at telomeric regions where it contributes to epigenetic silencing of clonally variant virulence genes. Apart from telomeres, PfSir2a also accumulates in the nucleolus, which harbours the developmentally regulated ribosomal RNA genes. Here we investigate the nucleolar function of PfSir2a and demonstrate that PfSir2a fine-tunes ribosomal RNA gene transcription. Using a parasite line in which PfSir2a has been disrupted, we observe that histones near the transcription start sites of all ribosomal RNA genes are hyperacetylated and that transcription of ribosomal RNA genes is upregulated. Complementation of the PfSir2a-disrupted parasites restores the ribosomal RNA levels, whereas PfSir2a overexpression in wild-type parasites decreases ribosomal RNA synthesis. Furthermore, we observe that PfSir2a modulation of ribosomal RNA synthesis is linked to an altered number of daughter merozoites and the parasite multiplication rate. These findings provide new insights into an epigenetic mechanism that controls malaria parasite proliferation and virulence.

      A Plasmodium falciparum Histone Deacetylase Regulates Antigenic Variation and Gametocyte Conversion
      Coleman, Bradley I.; Skillman, Kristen M.; Jiang, Rays H. Y.; Childs, Lauren M.; Altenhofen, Lindsey M.; Ganter, Markus; Leung, Yvette; Goldowitz, Ilana; Kafsack, Bjorn F. C.; Marti, Matthias; Llinas, Manuel; Buckee, Caroline O.; Duraisingh, Manoj T.
      Cell Host & Microbe (2014), 16 (2), 177-186CODEN: CHMECB; ISSN:1931-3128. (Elsevier Inc.)
      The asexual forms of the malaria parasite Plasmodium falciparum are adapted for chronic persistence in human red blood cells, continuously evading host immunity using epigenetically regulated antigenic variation of virulence-assocd. genes. Parasite survival on a population level also requires differentiation into sexual forms, an obligatory step for further human transmission. We reveal that the essential nuclear gene, P. falciparum histone deacetylase 2 (PfHda2), is a global silencer of virulence gene expression and controls the frequency of switching from the asexual cycle to sexual development. PfHda2 depletion leads to dysregulated expression of both virulence-assocd. var genes and PfAP2-g, a transcription factor controlling sexual conversion, and is accompanied by increases in gametocytogenesis. Math. modeling further indicates that PfHda2 has likely evolved to optimize the parasite's infectious period by achieving low frequencies of virulence gene expression switching and sexual conversion. This common regulation of cellular transcriptional programs mechanistically links parasite transmissibility and virulence.

      Apicidin: a novel antiprotozoal agent that inhibits parasite histone deacetylase
      Darkin-Rattray, Sandra J.; Gurnett, Anne M.; Myers, Robert W.; Dulski, Paula M.; Crumley, Tami M.; Allocco, John J.; Cannova, Christine; Meinke, Peter T.; Colletti, Steven L.; et al.
      Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America (1996), 93 (23), 13143-13147CODEN: PNASA6; ISSN:0027-8424. (National Academy of Sciences)
      A novel fungal metabolite, apicidin [cyclo(N-O-methyl-L-tryptophanyl-L-isoleucinyl-D-pipecolinyl-L-2-amino-8-oxodecanoyl)], that exhibits potent, broad spectrum antiprotozoal activity in vitro against Apicomplexan parasites has been identified. It is also orally and parenterally active in vivo against Plasmodium berghei malaria in mice. Many Apicomplexan parasites cause serious, life-threatening human and animal diseases, such as malaria, cryptosporidiosis, toxoplasmosis, and coccidiosis, and new therapeutic agents are urgently needed. Apicidin's antiparasitic activity appears to be due to low nanomolar inhibition of Apicomplexan histone deacetylase (HDA), which induces hyperacetylation of histones in treated parasites. The acetylation-deacetylation of histones is thought to play a central role in transcriptional control in eukaryotic cells. Other known HDA inhibitors were also evaluated and found to possess antiparasitic activity, suggesting that HDA is an attractive target for the development of novel antiparasitic agents.

      Anti-malarial effect of histone deacetylation inhibitors and mammalian tumour cytodifferentiating agents
      Andrews, Kathy T.; Walduck, Anna; Kelso, Mike J.; Fairlie, David P.; Saul, Allan; Parsons, Peter G.
      International Journal for Parasitology (2000), 30 (6), 761-768CODEN: IJPYBT; ISSN:0020-7519. (Elsevier Science Ltd.)
      The histones of Plasmodium falciparum represent a potential new target for anti-malarial compds.       Antimalarial activity of phenylthiazolyl-bearing hydroxamate-based histone deacetylase inhibitors
      Dow, Geoffrey S.; Chen, Yufeng; Andrews, Katherine T.; Cardiha, Diana; Gerena, Lucia; Gettayacamin, Montip; Johnson, Jacob; Li, Qigui; Melendez, Victor; Obaldia, Nicanor, III; Tran, Thanh N.; Kozikowski, Alan P.

In in-vitro growth inhibition assays approx. 50 analogs were evaluated against four drug resistant strains of Plasmodium falciparum. The range of 50% inhibitory concns. (IC50s) was 0.0005 to >1 Î¼M. Five analogs exhibited IC50s of <3 nM, and three of these exhibited selectivity indexes of >600. The most potent compd., WR301801 (YC-2-88) was shown to cause hyperacetylation of P. falciparum histones, which is a marker for HDAC inhibition in eukaryotic cells. The compd. also inhibited malarial and mammalian HDAC activity in functional assays at low nanomolar concns. WR301801 did not exhibit cures in P. berghei-infected mice at oral doses as high as 640 mg/kg/day for 3 days or in P. falciparum-infected Aotus lemurinus monkeys at oral doses of 32 mg/kg/day for 3 days, despite high relative bioavailability. The failure of monotherapy in mice may be due to a short half-life, since the compd. was rapidly hydrolyzed to an inactive acid metabolite by loss of its hydroxamate group in vitro (half-life of 11 min in mouse microsomes) and in vivo (half-life in mice of 3.5 h after a single oral dose of 50 mg/kg). However, WR301801 exhibited cures in P. berghei-infected mice when combined at doses of 52 mg/kg/day orally with subcurative doses of chloroquine. Next-generation HDACIs with greater metabolic stability than WR301801 may be useful as antimalarials if combined appropriately with conventional antimalarial drugs.

      Antimalarial activity of the anticancer histone deacetylase inhibitor SB939
      Sumanadasa, Subathdrage D. M.; Goodman, Christopher D.; Lucke, Andrew J.; Skinner-Adams, Tina; Sahama, Ishani; Haque, Ashraful; Do, Tram Anh; McFadden, Geoffrey I.; Fairlie, David P.; Andrews, Katherine T.
      Antimicrobial Agents and Chemotherapy (2012), 56 (7), 3849-3856CODEN: AMACCQ; ISSN:0066-4804. (American Society for Microbiology)
      Histone deacetylase (HDAC) enzymes posttranslationally modify lysines on histone and nonhistone proteins and play crucial roles in epigenetic regulation and other important cellular processes. HDAC inhibitors (e.g., suberoylanilide hydroxamic acid [SAHA; also known as vorinostat]) are used clin. to treat some cancers and are under investigation for use against many other diseases. Development of new HDAC inhibitors for noncancer indications has the potential to be accelerated by piggybacking onto cancer studies, as several HDAC inhibitors have undergone or are undergoing clin. trials. One such compd., SB939, is a new orally active hydroxamate-based HDAC inhibitor with an improved pharmacokinetic profile compared to that of SAHA. In this study, the in vitro and in vivo antiplasmodial activities of SB939 were investigated. SB939 was found to be a potent inhibitor of the growth of Plasmodium falciparum asexual-stage parasites in vitro (50% inhibitory concn. [IC50], 100 to 200 nM), causing hyperacetylation of parasite histone and nonhistone proteins. In combination with the aspartic protease inhibitor lopinavir, SB939 displayed additive activity. SB939 also potently inhibited the in vitro growth of exoerythrocytic-stage Plasmodium parasites in liver cells (IC50, â¼150 nM), suggesting that inhibitor targeting to multiple malaria parasite life cycle stages may be possible. In an exptl. in vivo murine model of cerebral malaria, orally administered SB939 significantly inhibited P. berghei ANKA parasite growth, preventing development of cerebral malaria-like symptoms. These results identify SB939 as a potent new antimalarial HDAC inhibitor and underscore the potential of investigating next-generation anticancer HDAC inhibitors as prospective new drug leads for treatment of malaria.

      Effect of clinically approved HDAC inhibitors on Plasmodium, Leishmania and Schistosoma parasite growth
      Chua Ming Jang; Arnold Megan S J; Skinner-Adams Tina S; Xu Weijun; Fairlie David P; Lancelot Julien; Pierce Raymond J; Lamotte Suzanne; Spath Gerald F; Prina Eric; Andrews Katherine T
      International journal for parasitology. Drugs and drug resistance (2017), 7 (1), 42-50 ISSN:.
      Malaria, schistosomiasis and leishmaniases are among the most prevalent tropical parasitic diseases and each requires new innovative treatments.       Non-Peptide Macrocyclic Histone Deacetylase Inhibitors Derived from Tricyclic Ketolide Skeleton
      Mwakwari, Sandra C.; Guerrant, William; Patil, Vishal; Khan, Shabana I.; Tekwani, Babu L.; Gurard-Levin, Zachary A.; Mrksich, Milan; Oyelere, Adegboyega K.
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2010), 53 (16), 6100-6111CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      Inhibition of histone deacetylase (HDAC) function is a validated therapeutic strategy for cancer treatment. Of the several structurally distinct small mol. histone deacetylase inhibitors (HDACi) reported, macrocyclic depsipeptides possess the most complex cap groups and have demonstrated excellent HDAC inhibition potency and isoform selectivity. Unfortunately, the development of macrocyclic depsipeptides has been hampered in part because of development problems characteristic of large peptides and the complex reaction schemes required for their synthesis. Herein we report that tricyclic ketolide TE-802 is an excellent mimetic for the peptide backbone of macrocyclic HDACi. Compds. derived from this template are particularly selective against HDACs 1 and 2 with nanomolar inhibitory activity. Interrogation of the assocn. between a subset of these compds. and key HDAC isoforms, using AutoDock, enables a mol. description of the interaction between the HDAC enzyme's outer rim and the inhibitors' macrocyclic cap group that are responsible for compd. affinity and presumably isoform selectivity.

      Chen, Feifei; Di, Hongxia; Wang, Youxin; Cao, Qiao; Xu, Bin; Zhang, Xue; Yang, Nana; Liu, Guijie; Yang, Cai-Guang; Xu, Yong; Jiang, Hualiang; Lian, Fulin; Zhang, Naixia; Li, Jian; Lan, Lefu

      Mu, Jianbing; Myers, Rachel A.; Jiang, Hongying; Liu, Shengfa; Ricklefs, Stacy; Waisberg, Michael; Chotivanich, Kesinee; Wilairatana, Polrat; Krudsood, Srivicha; White, Nicholas J.; Udomsangpetch, Rachanee; Cui, Liwang; Ho, May; Ou, Fengzhen; Li, Haibo; Song, Jianping; Li, Guoqiao; Wang, Xinhua; Seila, Suon; Sokunthea, Sreng; Socheat, Duong; Sturdevant, Daniel E.; Porcella, Stephen F.; Fairhurst, Rick M.; Wellems, Thomas E.; Awadalla, Philip; Su, Xin-zhuan

Here, the authors report the first Plasmodium GWAS, which included 189 culture-adapted P. falciparum parasites genotyped using a custom-built Affymetrix mol. inversion probe 3K malaria panel array with a coverage of â¼1 SNP per 7 kb. This study provides valuable tools and insight into the P. falciparum genome.

      A surrogate marker of piperaquine-resistant Plasmodium falciparum malaria: a phenotype-genotype association study
      Witkowski, Benoit; Duru, Valentine; Khim, Nimol; Ross, Leila S.; Saintpierre, Benjamin; Beghain, Johann; Chy, Sophy; Kim, Saorin; Ke, Sopheakvatey; Kloeung, Nimol; Eam, Rotha; Khean, Chanra; Ken, Malen; Loch, Kaknika; Bouillon, Anthony; Domergue, Anais; Ma, Laurence; Bouchier, Christiane; Leang, Rithea; Huy, Rekol; Nuel, Gregory; Barale, Jean-Christophe; Legrand, Eric; Ringwald, Pascal; Fidock, David A.; Mercereau-Puijalon, Odile; Ariey, Frederic; Menard, Didier
      Lancet Infectious Diseases (2017), 17 (2), 174-183CODEN: LIDABP; ISSN:1473-3099. (Elsevier Ltd.)
      Western Cambodia is the epicentre of Plasmodium falciparum multidrug resistance and is facing high rates of dihydroartemisinin-piperaquine treatment failures. Genetic tools to detect the multidrug-resistant parasites are needed. Artemisinin resistance can be tracked using the K13 mol. marker, but no marker exists for piperaquine resistance. We aimed to identify genetic markers of piperaquine resistance and study their assocn. with dihydroartemisinin-piperaquine treatment failures. We obtained blood samples from Cambodian patients infected with P falciparum and treated with dihydroartemisinin-piperaquine. Patients were followed up for 42 days during the years 2009-15. We established in-vitro and ex-vivo susceptibility profiles for a subset using piperaquine survival assays. We detd. whole-genome sequences by Illumina paired-reads sequencing, copy no. variations by qPCR, RNA concns. by qRT-PCR, and protein concns. by immunoblotting. Fisher's exact and non-parametric Wilcoxon rank-sum tests were used to identify significant differences in single-nucleotide polymorphisms or copy no. variants, resp., for differential distribution between piperaquine-resistant and piperaquine-sensitive parasite lines. Whole-genome exon sequence anal. of 31 culture-adapted parasite lines assocd. amplification of the plasmepsin 2-plasmepsin 3 gene cluster with in-vitro piperaquine resistance. Ex-vivo piperaquine survival assay profiles of 134 isolates correlated with plasmepsin 2 gene copy no. In 725 patients treated with dihydroartemisinin-piperaquine, multicopy plasmepsin 2 in the sample collected before treatment was assocd. with an adjusted hazard ratio (aHR) for treatment failure of 20Â·4 (95% CI 9Â·1-45Â·5, p<0Â·0001). Multicopy plasmepsin 2 predicted dihydroartemisinin-piperaquine failures with 0Â·94 (95% CI 0Â·88-0Â·98) sensitivity and 0Â·77 (0Â·74-0Â·81) specificity. Anal. of samples collected across the country from 2002 to 2015 showed that the geog. and temporal increase of the proportion of multicopy plasmepsin 2 parasites was highly correlated with increasing dihydroartemisinin-piperaquine treatment failure rates (r=0Â·89 [95% CI 0Â·77-0Â·95], p<0Â·0001, Spearman's coeff. of rank correlation). Dihydroartemisinin-piperaquine efficacy at day 42 fell below 90% when the proportion of multicopy plasmepsin 2 parasites exceeded 22%. Piperaquine resistance in Cambodia is strongly assocd. with amplification of plasmepsin 2-3, encoding Hb-digesting proteases, regardless of the location. Multicopy plasmepsin 2 constitutes a surrogate mol. marker to track piperaquine resistance. A mol. toolkit combining plasmepsin 2 with K13 and mdr1 monitoring should provide timely information for antimalarial treatment and containment policies. Institut Pasteur in Cambodia, Institut Pasteur Paris, National Institutes of Health, WHO, Agence Nationale de la Recherche, Investissement d'Avenir program, Laboratoire d'Excellence Integrative "Biol. of Emerging Infectious Diseases".

      High efficiency transfection of Plasmodium berghei facilitates novel selection procedures
      Janse, Chris J.; Franke-Fayard, Blandine; Mair, Gunnar R.; Ramesar, Jai; Thiel, Corinna; Engelmann, Sabine; Matuschewski, Kai; van Gemert, Geert Jan; Sauerwein, Robert W.; Waters, Andrew P.

      The use of transfection in the study of the biol. of malaria parasites has been limited due to poor transfection efficiencies (frequency of 10-6 to 10-9) and a paucity of selectable markers. Here, a new method of transfection, using non-viral Nucleofector technol., is described for the rodent parasite Plasmodium berghei. The transfection efficiency obtained (episomal and targeted integration into the genome) is in the range of 10-2 to 10-3. Such high transfection efficiency strongly reduces the time, no. of lab. animals and amt. of materials required to generate transfected parasites. Since there is no need to use a drug-selectable marker, this method increases the (low) no. of selectable markers available for transformation of P. berghei and can in principle be extended to utilize addnl. FP. Furthermore the FACS-selected, FP-expressing parasites may serve as easily visualized ref. lines that may still be genetically manipulated with the existing drug-selectable markers. The combination of enhanced transfection efficiency and a versatile rodent model provides a basis for the further development of novel tools for high throughput genome manipulation.

      Targeting Plasmodium PI(4)K to eliminate malaria
      McNamara, Case W.; Lee, Marcus C. S.; Lim, Chek Shik; Lim, Siau Hoi; Roland, Jason; Nagle, Advait; Simon, Oliver; Yeung, Bryan K. S.; Chatterjee, Arnab K.; McCormack, Susan L.; Manary, Micah J.; Zeeman, Anne-Marie; Dechering, Koen J.; Kumar, T. R. Santha; Henrich, Philipp P.; Gagaring, Kerstin; Ibanez, Maureen; Kato, Nobutaka; Kuhen, Kelli L.; Fischli, Christoph; Rottmann, Matthias; Plouffe, David M.; Bursulaya, Badry; Meister, Stephan; Rameh, Lucia; Trappe, Joerg; Haasen, Dorothea; Timmerman, Martijn; Sauerwein, Robert W.; Suwanarusk, Rossarin; Russell, Bruce; Renia, Laurent; Nosten, Francois; Tully, David C.; Kocken, Clemens H. M.; Glynne, Richard J.; Bodenreider, Christophe; Fidock, David A.; Diagana, Thierry T.; Winzeler, Elizabeth A.
      Nature (London, United Kingdom) (2013), 504 (7479), 248-253CODEN: NATUAS; ISSN:0028-0836. (Nature Publishing Group)
      Achieving the goal of malaria elimination will depend on targeting Plasmodium pathways essential across all life stages. Here the authors identify a lipid kinase, phosphatidylinositol-4-OH kinase (PI(4)K), as the target of imidazopyrazines, a new antimalarial compd. class that inhibits the intracellular development of multiple Plasmodium species at each stage of infection in the vertebrate host. Imidazopyrazines demonstrate potent preventive, therapeutic, and transmission-blocking activity in rodent malaria models, are active against blood-stage field isolates of the major human pathogens P. falciparum and P. vivax, and inhibit liver-stage hypnozoites in the simian parasite P. cynomolgi. The authors show that imidazopyrazines exert their effect through inhibitory interaction with the ATP-binding pocket of PI(4)K, altering the intracellular distribution of phosphatidylinositol-4-phosphate. Collectively, the authors' data define PI(4)K as a key Plasmodium vulnerability, opening up new avenues of target-based discovery to identify drugs with an ideal activity profile for the prevention, treatment and elimination of malaria.

      JNJ-26481585, a Novel "Second-Generation" Oral Histone Deacetylase Inhibitor, Shows Broad-Spectrum Preclinical Antitumoral Activity
      Arts, Janine; King, Peter; Marien, Ann; Floren, Wim; Belien, Ann; Janssen, Lut; Pilatte, Isabelle; Roux, Bruno; Decrane, Laurence; Gilissen, Ron; Hickson, Ian; Vreys, Veronique; Cox, Eugene; Bol, Kees; Talloen, Willem; Goris, Ilse; Andries, Luc; Du Jardin, Marc; Janicot, Michel; Page, Martin; van Emelen, Kristof; Angibaud, Patrick
      Clinical Cancer Research (2009), 15 (22), 6841-6851CODEN: CCREF4; ISSN:1078-0432. (American Association for Cancer Research)
      Purpose: Histone deacetylase (HDAC) inhibitors have shown promising clin. activity in the treatment of hematol. malignancies, but their activity in solid tumor indications has been limited. Most HDAC inhibitors in clin. development only transiently induce histone acetylation in tumor tissue. Here, we sought to identify a "second-generation" class I HDAC inhibitor with prolonged pharmacodynamic response in vivo, to assess whether this results in superior antitumoral efficacy. Exptl. Design: To identify novel HDAC inhibitors with superior pharmacodynamic properties, we developed a preclin. in vivo tumor model, in which tumor cells have been engineered to express fluorescent protein dependent on HDAC1 inhibition, thereby allowing noninvasive real-time evaluation of the tumor response to HDAC inhibitors. Results: In vivo pharmacodynamic anal. of 140 potent pyrimidyl-hydroxamic acid analogs resulted in the identification of JNJ-26481585. Once daily oral administration of JNJ-26481585 induced continuous histone H3 acetylation. The prolonged pharmacodynamic response translated into complete tumor growth inhibition in Ras mutant HCT116 colon carcinoma xenografts, whereas 5-fluorouracil was less active. JNJ-26481585 also fully inhibited the growth of C170HM2 colorectal liver metastases, whereas again 5-fluorouracil/Leucovorin showed modest activity. Further characterization revealed that JNJ-26481585 is a pan-HDAC inhibitor with marked potency toward HDAC1 (IC50, 0.16 nmol/L). Conclusions: The potent antitumor activity as a single agent in preclin. models combined with its favorable pharmacodynamic profile makes JNJ-26481585 a promising "second-generation" HDAC inhibitor. The compd. is currently in clin. studies, to evaluate its potential applicability in a broad spectrum of both solid and hematol. malignancies.

      Prommana, Parichat; Uthaipibull, Chairat; Wongsombat, Chayaphat; Kamchonwongpaisan, Sumalee; Yuthavong, Yongyuth; Knuepfer, Ellen; Holder, Anthony A.; Shaw, Philip J.
      PLoS One (2013), 8 (8), e73783CODEN: POLNCL; ISSN:1932-6203. (Public Library of Science)
      Conventional reverse genetic approaches for study of Plasmodium malaria parasite gene function are limited, or not applicable. Hence, new inducible systems are needed. Here we describe a method to control P. falciparum gene expression in which target genes bearing a glmS ribozyme in the 3' untranslated region are efficiently knocked down in transgenic P. falciparum parasites in response to glucosamine inducer. Using reporter genes, we show that the glmS ribozyme cleaves reporter mRNA in vivo leading to redn. in mRNA expression following glucosamine treatment. Glucosamine-induced ribozyme activation led to efficient redn. of reporter protein, which could be rapidly reversed by removing the inducer. The glmS ribozyme was validated as a reverse-genetic tool by integration into the essential gene and antifolate drug target dihydrofolate reductase-thymidylate synthase (PfDHFR-TS). Glucosamine treatment of transgenic parasites led to rapid and efficient knockdown of PfDHFR-TS mRNA and protein. PfDHFR-TS knockdown led to a growth/arrest mutant phenotype and hypersensitivity to pyrimethamine. The glmS ribozyme may thus be a tool for study of essential genes in P. falciparum and other parasite species amenable to transfection.
